  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_711438b5-476b-426b-b6c7-07f4879b9385'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer14902373078587167941">Body-snatching has never been so heartwarming . . .<br/><br/><i>The Humans</i> is a funny, compulsively readable novel about alien abduction, mathematics, and that most interesting subject of all: ourselves. Combine Douglas Adams’s irreverent take on life, the universe, and everything with a genuinely moving love story, and you have some idea of the humor, originality, and poignancy of Mat</span>
  <span id="freeText14902373078587167941" style="display:none">Body-snatching has never been so heartwarming . . .<br /><br /><i>The Humans</i> is a funny, compulsively readable novel about alien abduction, mathematics, and that most interesting subject of all: ourselves. Combine Douglas Adams’s irreverent take on life, the universe, and everything with a genuinely moving love story, and you have some idea of the humor, originality, and poignancy of Matt Haig’s latest novel.<br /><br />Our hero, Professor Andrew Martin, is dead before the book even begins. As it turns out, though, he wasn’t a very nice man--as the alien imposter who now occupies his body discovers. Sent to Earth to destroy evidence that Andrew had solved a major mathematical problem, the alien soon finds himself learning more about the professor, his family, and “the humans” than he ever expected. When he begins to fall for his own wife and son--who have no idea he’s not the real Andrew--the alien must choose between completing his mission and returning home or finding a new home right here on Earth.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14902373078587167941" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">The basic premise is a little predictable, but it is so well written and complex on multiple levels that it quickly rises above the "obvious". If you <a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">The basic premise is a little predictable, but it is so well written and complex on multiple levels that it quickly rises above the "obvious". If you are open to exploring human behavior, motivation, and emotional bonding on a deeper level you will love this fun but also thoughtful book. If you are looking for pure Sci-Fi adventure, you will find other books more to your liking.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/609353873?book_show_action=true">May 07, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Karen Cole" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Karen Cole" href="/user/show/5844772-karen-cole">Karen Cole</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780857868756" href="/book/show/17790153-the-humans">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer609353873" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8046943612284373509">In August 2012 my brother committed suicide. In the dark days and weeks immediately after his death I read almost incessantly. I couldn't sleep because when I closed my eyes all I could see was his body (I had to go to the mortuary with my father to formally identify his body.) When I was awake I read so I could bear the raw grief ripping at my heart. I believe that it's thanks to books I survived those days, I'm not sure how I'd have coped without books giving me a respite from my at times over</span>
  <span id="freeText8046943612284373509" style="display:none">In August 2012 my brother committed suicide. In the dark days and weeks immediately after his death I read almost incessantly. I couldn't sleep because when I closed my eyes all I could see was his body (I had to go to the mortuary with my father to formally identify his body.) When I was awake I read so I could bear the raw grief ripping at my heart. I believe that it's thanks to books I survived those days, I'm not sure how I'd have coped without books giving me a respite from my at times overwhelming reality.<br /><br />The Humans wasn't published then but I wish it had been. It tells the story of an alien sent from the planet Vonnadoria to remove all evidence of the solving of the Riemann hypothesis (the key to prime numbers which guarantee a huge technological leap for mankind) by eminent Cambridge professor Andrew Martin. The Vonnadorians are horrified by this breakthrough as they see humans as a primitive, violent race not ready for the advancements the solving of the hypothesis will bring. An unnamed alien is therefore sent to Earth on a mission to ensure humankind remains unaware that this secret has been solved. He kills Martin then inhabits his body in order to infiltrate his life and erase all traces of his discovery, by removing all technological evidence and by killing anybody he may have told.<br />The first part of the book has several comic moments, the alien arrives knowing nothing of human life and finds himself naked and without language on a motorway. Matt Haig has held a magnifying glass to humans here and through the eyes of the alien Andrew we see our often irrational absurdity.<br />As the book progresses it becomes more poignant, Martin learns more about what it means to be human, thanks partly to a dog, peanut butter and Emily Dickinson. The 97 point list that features in the book is perhaps the book's shining moment, Matt's skillful blending of the emotional with the humorous means he avoids this list becoming saccharine and it is genuinely moving, my favourite point being "It's not the length of life that matters. It's the depth. But while burrowing, keep the sun above you."<br />The Humans is a beautifully written insight into what it means to be a human and how pain, sorrow and fear are a necessary part of that. With my grief not being a linear process there are days when I am hit again with an almost unbearable sadness. I know that on those days I will turn once more to The Humans. I don't ever feel suicidal but there are times when I question what it means to live. When I read The Humans I am given an answer.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8046943612284373509" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1019706528" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1019706528" />
    <a title="Riku Sayuj" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1651956-riku-sayuj"><img alt="Riku Sayuj" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1433433464p2/1651956.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1019706528?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Riku Sayuj" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Riku Sayuj" href="/user/show/1651956-riku-sayuj">Riku Sayuj</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1651956-riku-sayuj?shelf=genre-fiction">genre-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1019706528" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11347125161125911564"><br/><blockquote>
  <i>“I know that some of you reading this are convinced humans are a myth, but I am here to state that they do actually exist. For those that don’t know, a human is a real bipedal life form of midrange intelligence, living a largely deluded existence on a small waterlogged planet in a very lonely corner of the universe.”</i>
</blockquote><br/><br/><b>Encountering the Alien</b><br/><br/>This must be one of the most muted and personal alien invasion stories that you will come across. As the narrator admits, this is more magical realism than </span>
  <span id="freeText11347125161125911564" style="display:none"><br /><blockquote>
  <i>“I know that some of you reading this are convinced humans are a myth, but I am here to state that they do actually exist. For those that don’t know, a human is a real bipedal life form of midrange intelligence, living a largely deluded existence on a small waterlogged planet in a very lonely corner of the universe.”</i>
</blockquote><br /><br /><b>Encountering the Alien</b><br /><br />This must be one of the most muted and personal alien invasion stories that you will come across. As the narrator admits, this is more magical realism than science fiction in many ways.<br /><br />Haig displays a wise understanding of the human condition and exploits to the hilt the vantage point that he has given his narrator. Many anthropological, sociological and evolutionary texts often invite the reader to view the world as if it is being viewed from an alien sociologist/scientist’s perspective. Haig converts that into a novel length observation about the humans, and adds a twist: instead of observing only from a constant alien perspective, he makes the vantage point an ever shifting one - that of a continually evolving perspective that is moving closer and closer to that of the human. <br /><br />So we are given a picture of how alien the human race is at first glance, and how familiarity is the only way to reconcile with the many contradictions of the human condition, and how when you then look back, it is impossible to identify what exact elements turned you off in the beginning. While this is in an alien-human context in this book, this process is also applicable to any new encounter with an ‘alien’ culture among us humans too. And as many other sci-fi books demonstrate, this is a fully two-way street. You cannot meet an alien culture that you cannot love - if you don't, the fault must be more in you than in the culture.<br /><br />I have made this sound more philosophical than it is. And I am not doing the novel justice. Haig does all this with a very light touch, keeping a steady dose of unassuming British humor. The plot is kept entertaining though its overall nature is very predictable, but as Haig takes pains to show, the beauty is in the small details. That is what makes the human species worth preserving. At first glance no alien race would be able to resist the temptation to exterminate a dangerous, almost rabid, species like ours. Given time, we just might charm them though.<br /><br />Disclaimer: The novel makes a good case for the humans. I am not entirely convinced. <br /><br /><br /><br /><b>Post Script: Advice From a Distance</b><br /><br />The narrator decides to give some advice to fellow humans and comes up with some cliched (yet wise) gems. A selection:<br /><br />1. Shame is a shackle. Free yourself.<br />2. Don’t worry about your abilities. You have the ability to love. That is enough.<br />3. Be nice to other people. At the universal level, they are you.<br />4. Technology won’t save humankind. Humans will.<br />6. Be curious. Question everything. A present fact is just a future fiction.<br />7. Irony is fine, but not as fine as feeling.<br />8. Peanut butter sandwiches go perfectly well with a glass of white wine. Don’t let anyone tell you otherwise.<br />9. Sometimes, to be yourself you will have to forget yourself and become something else. Your character is not a fixed thing. You will sometimes have to move to keep up with it.<br />10. History is a branch of mathematics. So is literature. Economics is a branch of religion.<br />11. Sex can damage love but love can’t damage sex.<br />12. The news should start with mathematics, then poetry, and move down from there.<br />13. You shouldn’t have been born. Your existence is as close to impossible as can be. To dismiss the impossible is to dismiss yourself.<br />14. Your life will have 25,000 days in it. Make sure you remember some of them.<br />15. The road to snobbery is the road to misery. And vice versa.<br />16. Tragedy is just comedy that hasn’t come to fruition. One day we will laugh at this. We will laugh at everything.<br />17. Wear clothes, by all means, but remember they are clothes.<br />18. One life form’s gold is another life form’s tin can.<br />19. Read poetry. Especially poetry by Emily Dickinson. It might save you. Anne Sexton knows the mind, Walt Whitman knows grass, but Emily Dickinson knows everything.<br />22. Don’t worry about being angry. Worry when being angry becomes impossible. Because then you have been consumed.<br />23. Happiness is not out here. It is in there.<br />24. New technology, on Earth, just means something you will laugh at in five years. Value the stuff you won’t laugh at in five years. Like love. Or a good poem. Or a song. Or the sky.<br />25. There is only one genre in fiction. The genre is called ‘book’.<br />28. Your mother should write a novel. Encourage her.<br />29. If there is a sunset, stop and look at it. Knowledge is finite. Wonder is infinite.<br />30. Don’t aim for perfection. Evolution, and life, only happen through mistakes.<br />31. Failure is a trick of the light.<br />32. You are human. You will care about money. But realise it can’t make you happy because happiness is not for sale.<br />33. You are not the most intelligent creature in the universe. You are not even the most intelligent creature on your planet. The tonal language in the song of a humpback whale displays more complexity than the entire works of Shakespeare. It is not a competition. Well, it is. But don’t worry about it.<br />36. One day humans will live on Mars. But nothing there will be more exciting than a single overcast morning on Earth.<br />38. Walt Whitman was right about at least one thing. You will contradict yourself. You are large. You contain multitudes.<br />39. No one is ever completely right about anything. Anywhere.<br />40. Everyone is a comedy. If people are laughing at you they just don’t quite understand the joke that is themselves.<br />42. In a thousand years, if humans survive that long, everything you know will have been disproved. And replaced by even bigger myths.<br />43. Everything matters.<br />44. You have the power to stop time. You do it by kissing. Or listening to music. Music, by the way, is how you see things you can’t otherwise see. It is the most advanced thing you have. It is a superpower. Keep up with the bass guitar. You are good at it. Join a band.<br />46. A paradox. The things you don’t need to live – books, art, cinema, wine and so on – are the things you need to live.<br />47. A cow is a cow even if you call it beef.<br />48. No two moralities match. Accept different shapes, so long as they aren’t sharp enough to hurt.<br />50. At some point, bad things are going to happen. Have someone to hold on to.<br />52. If you are laughing, check that you don’t really want to cry. And vice versa.<br />53. Don’t ever be afraid of telling someone you love them. There are things wrong with your world, but an excess of love is not one.<br />55. You are not the only species on Earth with technology. Look at ants. Really. Look. What they do with twigs and leaves is quite amazing.<br />57. There are a lot of idiots in your species. Lots and lots. You are not one of them. Hold your ground.<br />60. Obey your head. Obey your heart. Obey your gut. In fact, obey everything except commands.<br />61. One day, if you get into a position of power, tell people this: just because you can, it doesn’t mean you should. There is a power and a beauty in unproved conjectures, unkissed lips and unpicked flowers.<br />63. It’s not the technique, it’s the method. It’s not the words, it’s the melody.<br />64. Be alive. That is your supreme duty to the world.<br />65. Don’t think you know. Know you think.<br />66. As a black hole forms it creates an immense gamma-ray burst, blinding whole galaxies with light and destroying millions of worlds. You could disappear at any second. This one. Or this one. Or this one. Make sure, as often as possible, you are doing something you’d be happy to die doing.<br />67. War is the answer. To the wrong question.<br />73. No one will understand you. It is not, ultimately, that important. What is important is that you understand you.<br />74. A quark is not the smallest thing. The wish you have on your death-bed – to have worked harder – that is the smallest thing. Because it won’t be there.<br />75. Politeness is often fear. Kindness is always courage. But caring is what makes you human. Care more, become more human.<br />76. In your mind, change the name of every day to Saturday. And change the name of work to play.<br />77. When you watch the news and see members of your species in turmoil, do not think there is nothing you can do. But know it is not done by watching news.<br />78. You get up. You put on your clothes. And then you put on your personality. Choose wisely.<br />80. Language is euphemism. Love is truth.<br />81. You can’t find happiness looking for the meaning of life. Meaning is only the third most important thing. It comes after loving and being.<br />82. If you think something is ugly, look harder. Ugliness is just a failure of seeing.<br />83. A watched pot never boils. That is all you need to know about quantum physics.<br />85. The Dark Ages never ended.<br />86. To like something is to insult it. Love it or hate it. Be passionate. As civilisation advances, so does indifference. It is a disease. Immunise yourself with art. And love.<br />89. At the sub-atomic level, everything is complex. But you do not live at the sub-atomic level. You have the right to simplify. If you don’t, you will go insane.<br />90. But know this. Men are not from Mars. Women are not from Venus. Do not fall for categories. Everyone is everything. Every ingredient inside a star is inside you, and every personality that ever existed competes in the theatre of your mind for the main role.<br />91. You are lucky to be alive. Inhale and take in life’s wonders. Never take so much as a single petal of a single flower for granted.<br />92. If you have children and love one more than another, work at it. They will know, even if it’s by a single atom less. A single atom is all you need to make a very big explosion.<br />93. School is a joke. But go along with it, because you are very near to the punchline.<br />94. You don’t have to be an academic. You don’t have to be anything. Don’t force it. Feel your way, and don’t stop feeling your way until something fits. Maybe nothing will. Maybe you are a road, not a destination. That is fine. Be a road. But make sure it’s one with something to look at out of the window.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11347125161125911564" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_889147303" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/889147303" />
    <a title="B Schrodinger" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5448792-b-schrodinger"><img alt="B Schrodinger" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572325701p2/5448792.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/889147303?book_show_action=true">Mar 22, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="B Schrodinger" class="user" itemprop="url" name="B Schrodinger" href="/user/show/5448792-b-schrodinger">B Schrodinger</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781443423656" href="/book/show/17827166-the-humans">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5448792-b-schrodinger?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer889147303&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer889147303" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12924652654893012465">Firstly, I could not finish this. Although I did not hate the book there were too many aspects of the story that just annoyed me no end. <br/><br/>"The Humans" is the story of an alien who is sent to Earth to eliminate all traces of the newly found proof of the Reimann hypothesis, which is said to be too powerful and dangerous knowledge for an immature species as us. The alien possesses the mathematician who proves the hypothesis, a professor at a prestigious university, who is also having a lot of family</span>
  <span id="freeText12924652654893012465" style="display:none">Firstly, I could not finish this. Although I did not hate the book there were too many aspects of the story that just annoyed me no end. <br /><br />"The Humans" is the story of an alien who is sent to Earth to eliminate all traces of the newly found proof of the Reimann hypothesis, which is said to be too powerful and dangerous knowledge for an immature species as us. The alien possesses the mathematician who proves the hypothesis, a professor at a prestigious university, who is also having a lot of family problems. Good premise, but you can see where it is heading.<br /><br />My main problems with this book come down to the suspension of disbelief and the constant attempts at being witty and quirky. The plot is only a facade for looking at humanity from a differing perspective, and despite what many would say in other reviews, it's not unique and it's not a revelation.<br /><br />In my previous edit, I had a large pick at the scientific inaccuracies. And while they are quite valid and something that the author could have rectified easily, each is not much of a problem. There are plenty of books with wildly bad science that get away with it. It's just the pure number of them (and a little bit of me felt the author was trying to be clever by attempting to use science too) constantly jarred me. When you are rolling your eyes at the book you are reading every few pages, suspension of disbelief goes out the window.<br /><br />On one page the alien was a naive blank slate, the next he had some prior knowledge suddenly, the next he was using keys with no problem. The alien main character only seemed to be naive or uninformed at the convenience of the author to make some glib observation, not to drive the plot and not in a consistent manner with the premise.<br /><br />With all these inconsistencies and problems, I found it hard to read this clichéd and predictable story. I found the that the humour and wit of this book came down to the same inane memes that your aunt will share on Facebook: I hate Mondays humour, only my dog understands me humour, kids these days humour. The philosophical, worldly observations were as deep as those given on a motivational calendar. <br />It's a Hallmark book. <br /><br />EDIT: I edited this review because a lot of other reviewers were critical of it. And while they'll always feel a need to write a remark about how I didn't understand some crucial plot point in the book, I do agree that my original review was much snarkier and less constructive than it should be. And I have the image of Matt Haig reading this and I want it to be as constructive as possible. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12924652654893012465" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Don't let anyone tell you otherwise...</i><br/><br/>Funny, hilarious, sad, loving and wise story with a message.   <span id="freeText16170274973673759197" style="display:none"><i>Peanut butter sandwiches go perfectly well with a glass of white wine. Don't let anyone tell you otherwise...</i><br /><br />Funny, hilarious, sad, loving and wise story with a message. An alien comes to earth to destroy evidence of Professor Andrew Martin who has supposedly solved a major mathematical problem. Martin is killed and the alien takes his place. At first he looks with disdain and surprise at the 'weak' and 'unwise' 'Humans'. But then he starts liking and loving his wife, gets involved in the strained life of his growingup son to help him, and he likes the dog :-) And then he finds out life on earth is not so bad, although 'the humans' could learn a lot.... There are some hilarious but also very wise philosophical statements and scenes in this book, which I will share later this weekend. I've read a couple of books from Matt Haig and he writes out-of-the-box, unusual stories, not typically to be arranged in some category. I like him!</span>

        <a title="Helene Jeppesen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Helene Jeppesen" href="/user/show/21992464-helene-jeppesen">Helene Jeppesen</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2381855343" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13385540516271226552">4.5/5 stars. <br/>I. Loved. This. Book.<br/>Granted, the plot is somewhat unrealistic and silly - it is after all about an alien who is put on Earth to complete a mission - but said alien’s observations on humans are spot on and very interesting as well as hilarious. We humans do weird things all the time without thinking about it, and it’s books like this one that make you realise that and make you stop to wonder why. <br/>I found this novel to be very clever, observant, and highly thought-provoking. Matt Hai</span>
  <span id="freeText13385540516271226552" style="display:none">4.5/5 stars. <br />I. Loved. This. Book.<br />Granted, the plot is somewhat unrealistic and silly - it is after all about an alien who is put on Earth to complete a mission - but said alien’s observations on humans are spot on and very interesting as well as hilarious. We humans do weird things all the time without thinking about it, and it’s books like this one that make you realise that and make you stop to wonder why. <br />I found this novel to be very clever, observant, and highly thought-provoking. Matt Haig is a clever author to have written it, and I loved how he - together with his multiple observations on the human race - was able to provide us with some truths on life and how we live it that you don’t often think about in everyday life. It didn’t at any point become to clichéd in my eyes - instead, it turned into an honest and heart-warming story that I truly appreciated. <br />Don’t go into this book with too high expectations. Take it for what it is, and maybe you’ll end up liking it as much as I did. I know that I’m for sure going to read more books of Matt Haig’s now, because he’s simply too good an author to not be further explored.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14330544053840489300">This was awesome. I chuckled so much and I barely ever do that while reading a book. "Andrew" as a character is absolutely genius. I don't really know what to say, y'all should just read it!!!</span>

<div id="review_717392448" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/717392448" />
    <a title="Mark Matthews" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5519555-mark-matthews"><img alt="Mark Matthews" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473338582p2/5519555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/717392448?book_show_action=true">Sep 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mark Matthews" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark Matthews" href="/user/show/5519555-mark-matthews">Mark Matthews</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer717392448" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2578865418484427018"> The world is divided into those who have read this book and those who have not. Those who have read this book are shaking their heads in the affirmative right now.<br/><br/>It is not so much the story, but read it for that. It is not so much the characters, but read it for that too. It is for the statement it makes on the flawed yet wondrous nature of humans. This book will resonate with you long after you read it. (if not, we can't be friends.) You will be convinced the author himself is from another wo</span>
  <span id="freeText2578865418484427018" style="display:none"> The world is divided into those who have read this book and those who have not. Those who have read this book are shaking their heads in the affirmative right now.<br /><br />It is not so much the story, but read it for that. It is not so much the characters, but read it for that too. It is for the statement it makes on the flawed yet wondrous nature of humans. This book will resonate with you long after you read it. (if not, we can't be friends.) You will be convinced the author himself is from another world, sent here to give us some wisdom, but perhaps also fearful if we can handle it. I liken it to "Illusions - The Adventures of a Reluctant Messiah" by Richard Bach<br /><br />Yes, I loved this book and I am a better person for it. A beautiful book that made me cry. At times I feared it would become predictable, but there was just enough variance and certainly more than enough genius. A wonderful range of emotions. The prose was both beautiful and simple. How many times have we all wondered, "What would an alien think if they came to Earth and experienced this?" Well, this book provides an illuminating answer.<br /><br />Highly recomended. Get ready to highlight on your kindle or dog-ear your paperback.<br /><br />-Mark Matthews<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_2349860822" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2349860822" />
    <a title="Gabby" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26560207-gabby"><img alt="Gabby" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1482222518p2/26560207.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2349860822?book_show_action=true">Apr 03, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gabby" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gabby" href="/user/show/26560207-gabby">Gabby</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780857868787" href="/book/show/21265230-the-humans">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26560207-gabby?shelf=5-star-books">5-star-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26560207-gabby?shelf=adult">adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26560207-gabby?shelf=books-to-reread-in-the-future">books-to-reread-in-the-future</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26560207-gabby?shelf=cover-lust">cover-lust</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26560207-gabby?shelf=love-the-concept">love-the-concept</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26560207-gabby?shelf=read-in-2018">read-in-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26560207-gabby?shelf=read-in-one-day">read-in-one-day</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26560207-gabby?shelf=suicide">suicide</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26560207-gabby?shelf=all-time-favorites">all-time-favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26560207-gabby?shelf=favorite-male-characters">favorite-male-characters</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2349860822" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11097081257432306584">My full review: <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=shEKaEsk9_c'>https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=shEKa...</a><br/><br/><b>This fucking book!</b> I picked this book up because I saw it recommended by a booktuber, and I wasn't expecting that much going into it. But I can honestly say this is a new favorite book of all time. <b>This story follows an alien who gets put into the body of this man named Andrew Martin.</b> The reason he gets put in his body is because Andrew Martin has a made a mathematical breakthrough and the aliens have determined humans are not ready for the</span>
  <span id="freeText11097081257432306584" style="display:none">My full review: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=shEKaEsk9_c">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=shEKa...</a><br /><br /><b>This fucking book!</b> I picked this book up because I saw it recommended by a booktuber, and I wasn't expecting that much going into it. But I can honestly say this is a new favorite book of all time. <b>This story follows an alien who gets put into the body of this man named Andrew Martin.</b> The reason he gets put in his body is because Andrew Martin has a made a mathematical breakthrough and the aliens have determined humans are not ready for the way this discovery would change the world. His mission is to delete all evidence that this mathmateical theory was solved, and kill anyone who knows it was solved. This book started off <b>fucking hilarious</b>. The way this alien views humanity <i>cracks me up</i> because he doesn't understand anything about our people or our world, and seeing him experience humanity for the first time was so entertaining. He is able to communicate with his alien masters back at home, and some of the things they have to say about humanity are brutally honest and pessimistic but nonetheless true, like this: <br /><br /><i>
  <blockquote>"The humans are an arrogant species, defined by violence and greed. They have taken their home planet, the only one they have access to, and placed it on the road to destruction. They have created a world of divisions and categories and have continually failed to see the similarities between themselves. They have developed technology at a rate too fast for human psychology to keep up with, and yet they still pursue advancement for advancement's sake, and for the pursuit of money and fame they all crave so much."</blockquote>
</i> <br /><br />This book is full of thought-provoking statements about what it means to be human, and what love is, and human life and death. One of my favorites is this one: <i>"A human life is on average 80 Earth years or around 30,000 Earth days. Which means they are born, they make some friends, eat a few meals, they get married, or they don’t get married, have a child or two, or not, drink a few thousand glasses of wine, have sexual intercourse a few times, discover a lump somewhere, feel a bit of regret, wonder where all the time went, know they should have done it differently, realise they would have done it the same, and then they die. Into the great black nothing. Out of space. Out of time. The most trivial of trivial zeroes. And that’s it, the full caboodle. All confined to the same mediocre planet.”</i> It's kind of depressing to think about.<br /><br /><i>
  <blockquote>"Dark matter is needed to hold galaxies together. Your mind is a Galaxy. More dark than light. But the light makes it worthwhile."</blockquote>
</i><br /><br />This book kind of reminds me of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23677341.We_Are_the_Ants" title="We Are the Ants by Shaun David Hutchinson" rel="nofollow noopener">We Are the Ants</a> in that way. It's a coming of age story that involves aliens... and it has an extremely negative and pessimistic view of the world and humanity, until the main character slowly reaches an arc when they realize how beautiful and wonderful and meaningful life on Earth can be. It has the same overall message, which is probably why I loved this book so much. <br /><br /><i>
  <blockquote>“You shouldn’t have been born. Your existence is as close to impossible as can be. To dismiss the impossible is to dismiss yourself.”</blockquote>
</i><br /><br />This book had me laughing and crying, and I just fucking loved it so much. <b>This book had quotes that inspired me and made me question my own beliefs and gave me a new perspective on things.</b> <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[That part was so beautiful when the alien actually saved Gulliver from killing himself. Their relationship was so cute, and so important. I also loved watching the way he fell in love with Isobel, and he got to watch her fall in love with him again too. It was just so beautifully written. That quote about how humans couldn't possibly exist, but we do, and so it makes sense for humans to believe in God too actually made me stop and think for a minute. I'm not a religious person, but seeing that quote actually made me think for a minute, and see things in a new perspective. I just love how in the beginning, he questioned everything and thought humans were the dumbest species to ever exist, and then he grew so fond of humans - it was such a beautiful thing to see him fall in love with humanity. I think, like the main character, I tend to have a pessimistic view on everything, and it was so beautiful to watch his pessimism turn into admiration. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> I loved it, 10/10 would recommend. If you haven't read this book, please read this fucking book. <b>Definitely a new all time favorite of mine.</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="11097081257432306584" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Swaroop Kanti" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/100264072-swaroop-kanti"><img alt="Swaroop Kanti" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594460958p2/100264072.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2906292017?book_show_action=true">Jul 22, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Swaroop Kanti" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Swaroop Kanti" href="/user/show/100264072-swaroop-kanti">Swaroop Kanti</a>

        rated it

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_693123992" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/693123992" />
    <a title="Jill" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1788674-jill"><img alt="Jill" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1432902126p2/1788674.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/693123992?book_show_action=true">Aug 12, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jill" href="/user/show/1788674-jill">Jill</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781476737850" href="/book/show/18213516-the-humans">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1788674-jill?shelf=scifi">scifi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer693123992" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8714271652585721124">Premise: A socially awkward alien lands Earthside, naked on a Cambridge street…<br/><br/>And for a while, as Matt Haig builds from this premise, it’s funny! <i>The Humans</i> begins quite wonderfully with the arrival of an alien who can barely disguise his contempt towards humans and believes clothing is optional. The humor works because of our extraterrestrial narrator's terrific voice, which is matter of fact and superior. For example, the first piece of “literature” he reads is an issue of <i>Cosmopolitan</i>, which</span>
  <span id="freeText8714271652585721124" style="display:none">Premise: A socially awkward alien lands Earthside, naked on a Cambridge street…<br /><br />And for a while, as Matt Haig builds from this premise, it’s funny! <i>The Humans</i> begins quite wonderfully with the arrival of an alien who can barely disguise his contempt towards humans and believes clothing is optional. The humor works because of our extraterrestrial narrator's terrific voice, which is matter of fact and superior. For example, the first piece of “literature” he reads is an issue of <i>Cosmopolitan</i>, which leads to this pithy discussion of magazines: <i>
  <blockquote>Magazines are very popular, despite no human’s ever feeling better for having read them. Indeed, their chief purpose is to generate a sense of inferiority in the reader that consequently leads to a feeling of needing to buy something, which the humans then do, and then feel even worse, and so need to buy another magazine to see what they can buy next. It is an eternal and unhappy spiral that goes by the name of capitalism, and it is really quite popular.</blockquote>
</i> I would have liked an entire book of this: just a doofy alien in human form walking around the modern world trying—and failing—to make sense of it.<br /><br />Wishes never come true, however, so the last half of the book ditches the humor and devolves into New Age mumbo jumbo. The plot is unoriginal. Basically an alien comes to save Earth from too much knowledge, learns to appreciate humans, and abandons his old alien life to become a regular Joe Schmo. To supplement this lack of plot, Haig tries to explore the meaning of life through our bumbling old alien narrator, whose voice becomes instantly less charming as soon as he’s humanized.<br /><br />Blah. The moment someone expressly searches for the meaning of life is the moment I roll my eyes. Sure I find meaning aplenty in books, but it must arise organically through the natural interaction of characters and their environments. Even worse, the meaning of life discovered by Haig’s alien is more clichéd than a Hallmark card. There are a ton of lines like this at the end that made me figuratively gag: <i>
  <blockquote>To experience beauty on Earth, you needed to experience pain and to know mortality. That is why so much that is beautiful on this planet has to do with time passing and the Earth turning. Which might also explain why to look at such natural beauty was to also feel sadness and a craving for a life unlived.</blockquote>
</i> There are tons of quotes about how “love is life” and how “it’s only through our flaws that we can truly appreciate humankind." By this point, I’d mostly checked out, hoping the narrator had one last good joke in him about Catholics. (He didn’t.) <br /><br />The reason the alien comes to Earth is to destroy a mathematical proof. So here’s some reviewing math for <i>The Humans</i>: smart-alecky alien who makes fun of humankind (4 stars) + heavy-handed existential tripe (2 stars) = 3 stars. Boom, math.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8714271652585721124" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1875445965" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1875445965" />
    <a title="Chesca (thecrownedpages)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35614518-chesca-thecrownedpages"><img alt="Chesca (thecrownedpages)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585465596p2/35614518.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1875445965?book_show_action=true">Jan 17, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chesca (thecrownedpages)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chesca (thecrownedpages)" href="/user/show/35614518-chesca-thecrownedpages">Chesca (thecrownedpages)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35614518-chesca-thecrownedpages?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35614518-chesca-thecrownedpages?shelf=books-i-highly-recommend">books-i-highly-recommend</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35614518-chesca-thecrownedpages?shelf=read-in-2017">read-in-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35614518-chesca-thecrownedpages?shelf=general-fiction">general-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1875445965" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9814107472711829402">THERE IS HOPE IN HUMANITY.<br/><br/>Told through the romantic and curious perspective of an unnamed alien, Matt Haig’s <i>
  <b>The Humans</b>
</i> is an honest and mostly-optimistic view on humanity, showing that hope and greatness could be found in our imperfections as individuals and as a whole.<br/><br/>Let this book help you rethink about life and see its beauty. Give it a moment and you would not regret it!<br/><br/><blockquote>
  <i>
    <b> “Humans, as a rule, don't like mad people unless they are good at painting, and only then once they are dead.</b></i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText9814107472711829402" style="display:none">THERE IS HOPE IN HUMANITY.<br /><br />Told through the romantic and curious perspective of an unnamed alien, Matt Haig’s <i>
  <b>The Humans</b>
</i> is an honest and mostly-optimistic view on humanity, showing that hope and greatness could be found in our imperfections as individuals and as a whole.<br /><br />Let this book help you rethink about life and see its beauty. Give it a moment and you would not regret it!<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>
    <b> “Humans, as a rule, don't like mad people unless they are good at painting, and only then once they are dead. But the definition of mad, on Earth, seems to be very unclear and inconsistent. What is perfectly sane in one era turns out to be insane in another. The earliest humans walked around naked with no problem. Certain humans, in humid rainforests mainly, still do so. So, we must conclude that madness is sometimes a question of time, and sometimes of postcode. <br /><br />Basically, the key rule is, if you want to appear sane on Earth you have to be in the right place, wearing the right clothes, saying the right things, and only stepping on the right kind of grass.”</b>
  </i>
</blockquote><br />A Vonnadorian alien, taking the form of Professor Andrew Martin, was given the task to destroy the said man’s work, in which the Reimann Theory was proven, and everyone who knew about it. At the same time, he was to study the humans and test the aliens’ theories about our species.<br /><br />Andrew Martin, a mathematician at Cambridge University, was married before the alien took his identity. He had a wife, Isobel. Their marriage was in shambles, but they stayed together for the sake of appearances and because Isobel loved him. The two of them were famous in their fields of education, Isobel on history and Andrew on Mathematics, and as authors of bestselling books.<br /><br />Isobel cared so much that she was able to sacrifice the other aspects of her career just so she could take care of her family while Andrew was out working. She was just there in the background, ignored and unloved.<br /><br />With Andrew not caring for his family, their son, Gulliver, was left broken. He was bullied and people expected more from him because his father was a genius. And so, Gulliver was depressed and hated the world.<br /><br />When the alien entered Isobel and Gulliver’s lives, everything changed. It started when Andrew (the Alien) was found walking the streets naked. They were told that he just had an episode because of working too much. But Andrew was never the same again after the incident.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>
    <b> “6. Be curious. Question everything. A present fact is just a future fiction.”</b>
  </i>
</blockquote><br />The first thing that you should know about this tale is that it’s hilarious and philosophical. It is definitely not a sad story, but it will bring tears to your eyes not because it will break your heart, but because it will stitch up the seams it has. Being stitched up is not that nice, but it’s definitely worth it, right?<br /><br />The naïve protagonist, though very curious, was first and foremost disgusted by the way humans look, especially because of their protruding noses. He didn’t mean to act the opposite of the real Professor Andrew Martin who was heartless and never cared for his family. As the plot progressed, he was able to understand humans and be like them. In the end, I could say that he was the <i>most human</i> character in the book. He was selfless and caring, loving and protective. This imperfect, non-human creature changed the lives of the people around him, especially Isobel’s and Gulliver’s. He saved and destroyed, lied and confessed. He had come to appreciate all that he was able to experience whether they were good or bad. It was as if he found the greatest treasures of the earth in his journey. The way this person was developed was just exquisite.<br /><br />As for Isobel and Gulliver, these two were almost lifeless at the beginning of the story. They were living a life smudged in gray, and the new Andrew was able to fill their life with color as he tried to learn about his new body and identity. They became more loved and in the process became free of their burdens. With them, the Vonnadorian found a family he was able to call his own. Their relationship was a new beginning for each one of them, and in it they found what they’ve all lacked before: love.<br /><br />And then there was Newton, the family dog, who was the alien’s very first friend. It was just so adorable to find a man care for a dog as if they were equals. They shared peanut butter, walked outside, and “Andrew” told him about his secrets first. It was very realistic, in my opinion, because I talk to my dog too. Let’s just say that we could all find friendship in the most unlikely of people or creatures.<br /><br /><i>The Humans</i> was written in a very formal way and research-like, but was romantic. Frankenstein romantic, not Colleen Hoover-romantic, if that makes sense. You would notice that the tone of the story was very consistent from end to end, but it acquired more emotion as the events flowed. This is the type of book I’d love to call “the one with the fun and the feels.” Reading it was like watching the sky all day to see it change its colors from sunset to dawn.<br /><br />There were some moments wherein I almost panicked because there were math equations in the book, and I am never good in math, unless we’re talking about how much money I would need to survive. Fear not, my friend. You wouldn’t have to analyze the equations, and they would be explained. Also, whether you understood them or not, it would not affect the story. What you should expect is that there would be a lot of poetry and music. Maybe you could list them down in case you’ve never heard of the artistic pieces mentioned in it.<br /><br />If there’s one thing I would want to change in this story, it would be the ending. It was a meaningful closure, but it could have been better if I saw more of it, like what would happen when he arrived at that place. One more chapter would have been so amazing.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>
    <b>“And I knew the point of love right then.<br />The point of love was to help you survive.<br />The point was also to forget meaning. To stop looking and start living. The meaning was to hold the hand of someone you cared about and to live inside the present. Past and future were myths. The past was just the present that had died and the future would never exist anyway, because by the time we got to it the future would have turned into the present. The present was all there was.”</b>
  </i>
</blockquote><br />This book is for everyone, in case you doubt about getting a copy. If you love romance, it definitely has that, and it has the kind that’s bold, fragile, and honest all at the same time. It was as a flower blooming and learning to withstand its new surroundings. If you’re a nerd, then let’s all be nerds and learn through this book! If you’re depressed, let this thing make you happy! Let it give you a new perspective about life. Don’t let depression eat you up; fight it. I think this book will be wonderful for you. If you can’t feel anything towards life, let this story get that heart in your chest beating to life again. That thing just might need some light spilling into you. If you think you’re life is so good, perfect, or that you had it all planned out, let’s put you in the middle of an existential crisis! Grab a copy, and maybe you still have other things to do to make life even better.<br /><br />Ask me how I feel? I feel like a shooting star ready to set fire to the night sky.<br /><br />Lastly, because of this, I would love to check out Emily Dickinson’s poetry. Yes, I’ve never read any of them except those quoted in this embodiment of intelligence.<br /><br />Obviously, this is absolutely and highly recommended! Also, it goes to my favorites shelf.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>
    <b> “75. Politeness is often fear. Kindness is always courage. But caring is what makes you human. Care more, become more human.”</b>
  </i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="9814107472711829402" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10159338001145376811">Oh what a perfect book. In this deceptively simple story, an alien takes over the body of a mathematics professor in order to destroy any evidence his victim has found about an important and game-changing mathematical theorem. It is a simple task, except that he falls in love with the very idea of humans.<br/><br/>It's not sufficient to call this book heart-warming- it is funny, insightful, wise, and inspiring. Haig's observations on modern human life make the reader consider her own priorities and want t</span>
  <span id="freeText10159338001145376811" style="display:none">Oh what a perfect book. In this deceptively simple story, an alien takes over the body of a mathematics professor in order to destroy any evidence his victim has found about an important and game-changing mathematical theorem. It is a simple task, except that he falls in love with the very idea of humans.<br /><br />It's not sufficient to call this book heart-warming- it is funny, insightful, wise, and inspiring. Haig's observations on modern human life make the reader consider her own priorities and want to go out and start living each day as richly and fully as possible. <br /><br />There is a wonderful book trailer featuring some of the narrator's key observations of life and advice for humans, as read by fans. You can view the trailer here: <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://youtu.be/zX8V2OFHHbQ">http://youtu.be/zX8V2OFHHbQ</a><br /><br />My favourites?<br /><br />"In fiction there is no genre, there is only book."<br />"Irony is fine, but feeling is better." (I think I paraphrased that...)<br /><br />Fans of quirky books such as Mr Penumbra's 24 Hour Bookstore, The Rosie Project and the 100 Year Old Man Who Climbed Out the Window and Disappeared will love this book, as will teenagers, book lovers,dog lovers, philosophers, romantics, mathematicians, and anyone looking for a shot of witty inspiration. In some ways it's almost a Happiness Project, but fictional. This is a book I will return to time and again, and if I had teenage children, I would definitely gift it to them. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10159338001145376811" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2532958023" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2532958023" />

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="NAT.orious reads ☾" class="user" itemprop="url" name="NAT.orious reads ☾" href="/user/show/54624224-nat-orious-reads">NAT.orious reads ☾</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2532958023" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3926055292563770051"><b> 4 utterly mundane ★★★★✩</b><br/><i>
  <blockquote><b>This book is for you if…</b> you are ready for an unusual blend of genres; you will want to laugh out loud at your own silliness and you should be capable of self-irony, because boy was mine triggered.</blockquote>
</i><br/><br/><u>
  <b>⇝Overall</b>
</u><br/>I knew that this book was going to be <b>peculiar and funny in the best kind of ways</b> because I'v read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/33590076.How_to_Stop_Time' title='How to Stop Time by Matt Haig' rel='nofollow noopener'>How to Stop Time</a> by Matt a while ago. THE HUMANS is a homage to all the people who think they've got things figured out (sometimes me). The originality of the <b>plot </b></span>
  <span id="freeText3926055292563770051" style="display:none"><b> 4 utterly mundane ★★★★✩</b><br /><i>
  <blockquote><b>This book is for you if…</b> you are ready for an unusual blend of genres; you will want to laugh out loud at your own silliness and you should be capable of self-irony, because boy was mine triggered.</blockquote>
</i><br /><br /><u>
  <b>⇝Overall</b>
</u><br />I knew that this book was going to be <b>peculiar and funny in the best kind of ways</b> because I'v read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/33590076.How_to_Stop_Time" title="How to Stop Time by Matt Haig" rel="nofollow noopener">How to Stop Time</a> by Matt a while ago. THE HUMANS is a homage to all the people who think they've got things figured out (sometimes me). The originality of the <b>plot served the overall theme of the book very well</b>, which I - if I may be bold enough to assume - believe to be <b>setting our heads straight about the ridiculous trivialities</b> we humans love to worry about so much. Whenever somebody doubts your motives or ridicules you, remember: you do you (unless it's against human and animal rights and stuff)<br /><blockquote>
  <i>"No one will understand you. It is not, ultimately, that important. What is important is that you understand you.</i>
</blockquote><br />(spoken like a true Gryffindor Primary, I suppose) Matt indeed made me see that it's laughable at times. Humans lose sight of the important things in life as if it were a fucking lifestyle: the people we love, the pets that accompany us and the planet that nourishes us. Matt had very good advice to give on that:<br /><blockquote>
  <i>“Make sure, as often as possible, you are doing something you’d be happy to die doing.”</i>
</blockquote><br />Because in the end, people will always have shit to criticize, no matter how original or different you are and what good you could do with it. Don't let the word "crazy" hold you back:<br /><blockquote>
  <i>“Humans, as a rule, don’t like mad people unless they are good at painting, and only then once they are dead.”</i>
</blockquote><br />So, do that trip, eat that dessert, paint that painting, read that book, go to that art night, slap that hoe, kill that misogynist bastard... Okay I went <i>a bit</i> far there, but you get the point, I suppose.<br /><br /><u>
  <b>➺What’s happening.</b>
</u><br />The story kicks off with some <b>alien consciousness that inhabits the body of scientist</b> Andrew Martin. Said scientist just proved a groundbreaking mathematical hypothesis. The alien's mission is to stop this news from spreading and <b>making sure that humanity will not advance into the direction of this breakthrough</b>. They are simply not ready yet, or so judges the alien race from a faraway place in a galaxy humanity hasn't explored yet.<br /><br />Utterly shocked by the observations he makes about the humans <b><i>Alien Andrew</i> does his best to navigate through this strange world</b>. Ideally, nobody will ever notice the real Andrew doesn't exist anymore. His mission is to find the people who know about Andrew's discovery and to eliminate them. Running around Cambridge campus and scanning Cosmopolitan Magazines <b>with no clothes on does not exactly qualify as inconspicuous</b>.<br /><br />Funnily enough, his resulting experiences with public ridicule and rumour stand in harsh contrast to the main observation our favourite alien makes:<br /><blockquote>
  <i>As civilisation advances, so does indifference.</i>
</blockquote><br />As the story continues we readers witness that it is surprisingly hard for <i>Alien Andrew</i> to remain indifferent towards his new family.<br />_____________________<br /><b>
  <i> Writing quality + easy of reading</i>
</b> = 5*<br /><br /><b>
  <i>pace</i>
</b> = 4*<br /><br /><b>
  <i>plot development</i>
</b> = 5*<br /><br /><b>
  <i>characters</i>
</b> = 4*<br /><br /><b>
  <i>enjoyability</i>
</b> = 4*<br /><br /><b>
  <i>insightfulness</i>
</b> = 5*</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1709968333" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17470588472875770157">If you can't overlook scientific inaccuracies or take a step out of your belief zone, this book is probably not for you. I decided not to look too deep into the technicalities and to focus on the deeper message and as a result found I enjoyed this story. The unique perspective of an alien who goes through a range of human emotion adds depth to some of the issues and imperfections of our kind. He discovers, learns, loves, makes mistakes, and finds his way in this complicated world. I liked the dr</span>
  <span id="freeText17470588472875770157" style="display:none">If you can't overlook scientific inaccuracies or take a step out of your belief zone, this book is probably not for you. I decided not to look too deep into the technicalities and to focus on the deeper message and as a result found I enjoyed this story. The unique perspective of an alien who goes through a range of human emotion adds depth to some of the issues and imperfections of our kind. He discovers, learns, loves, makes mistakes, and finds his way in this complicated world. I liked the dry humor and the advice section of the book detailing some of his lessons learned. I liked that everything didn't line up for his success as a human because it made the story more relatable. I would have loved an epilogue or even a clear resolution to the dilemma he faced but I understand why it was done the way it was. All in all a solid read for me. Safety: There is cheating in this story. No rape/abuse.</span>

<div id="review_3076550645" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3076550645" />
    <a title="Collin " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/89672274-collin"><img alt="Collin " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605313863p2/89672274.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3076550645?book_show_action=true">Dec 07, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Collin " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Collin " href="/user/show/89672274-collin">Collin </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781786894663" href="/book/show/41135759-the-humans">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3076550645" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5305571232302654868">The Humans is about a race of aliens who find out that a human mathematician has made a breakthrough with a mathematical problem that will lead to a vast jump in technology. This species of aliens has already solved this problem and live a life of utopia. There is no death or pain, there are no wars or illness.<br/><br/>However, they do not trust the human species to follow the same course. So, they abduct the mathematician, clone and kill him. They then send the narrator of this story down to earth to de</span>
  <span id="freeText5305571232302654868" style="display:none">The Humans is about a race of aliens who find out that a human mathematician has made a breakthrough with a mathematical problem that will lead to a vast jump in technology. This species of aliens has already solved this problem and live a life of utopia. There is no death or pain, there are no wars or illness.<br /><br />However, they do not trust the human species to follow the same course. So, they abduct the mathematician, clone and kill him. They then send the narrator of this story down to earth to delete all traces of his breakthrough from the scientists computer, which is going to be difficult because although these aliens can clone our bodies and brains, they cannot clone what is stored in our brains, memories, and knowledge. So essentially our narrator is going in blind knowing nothing about us, our quirks and eccentricities, or even what a computer looks like.<br /><br />We then find out that our narrator does not even want this job, and to be honest is not really qualified for it. It is in fact a punishment for blasphemous talk against mathematical purity. Anyway, instead of being teleported into the mathematician’s office he is teleported into the middle of the highway where he is subsequently hit by a car, and with this accident we are led into a wonderful narrative which will make you stop and think what it is to be, and what makes us, human.<br /><br />At first the alien shares the views of his fellow Vonnadorians. That is that we are a race that is continually at war with itself, a race consumed with greed, hatred and violence. A race that is trapped in a never-ending circle of these emotions and acts. However, then he meets the wife and son of the mathematician and he discovers our greatest strength, love.<br /><br />He starts to see what this human species is capable of within it’s fleeting, blink of an eye life span. He realises that what his species has attained may not be the utopia they believe it to be. They have no illness, no death, no violence. But it is precisely this lack of mortality, illness, violence, that leads to them not being able to appreciate life, art, nature, love, the way humans do. <br /><br />At its heart this is a novel about love. As the novel progresses the alien slowly learns what it is to love, to be in love, the power of love. A force so powerful that he is willing to give up immortality and godlike powers to become a human. A life full of pain, hurt, sadness, loss, but also a life, though incredibly short, full of beauty, excitement, kindness, hope and love.<br /><br />Make sure you read the author’s acknowledgement page at the end. Wonderful. 4.5 Stars!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5305571232302654868" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText8259360673122123189" style="display:none">I don't mind taking a gamble on Audible Daily Deals - they're usually only a couple of quid and if they don't work out then it's less than the cost of a cup of coffee wasted. <br /><br />I usually know quite early doors if a book is going to excite me, bore me or simply get on my nerves. So far so good, but after every possible sentence uttered by everyone he/it meets is examined for its incongruity and/or weirdness the amusement factor wore off for me quick smart - and that was the end of that.<br /><br />The pity is that, had I been inclined to press on, there might be a good story here.   <span id="freeText7019055471280313087" style="display:none">A funny, clever, and unusual book, The Humans, explores what would happen if an alien came to earth and inhabited the body of an earthling and reacted to our life styles and rules for society. The alien comes to love the puny, delicate, mortal human wife and son he has inherited by killing their husband and father and taking his place. Needless to say, his fellow aliens are not happy with his failure to carry out his mission.</span>
